2:25:1267:0:14:72:372:700:12:Times:12:Times:The Consequences of Radical Reform: The French Revolution
2:26:1324:14:14:72:436:686:12:Times:12:Times:Daron Acemoglu, Davide Cantoni, Simon Johnson, and James A. Robinson
2:27:1392:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:NBER Working Paper No. 14831
2:28:1420:14:14:72:124:658:12:Times:12:Times:April 2009
2:29:1430:14:28:72:136:644:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. N23
2:30:1441:28:28:306:339:616:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:31:1449:28:14:72:540:588:12:Times:12:Times:The French Revolution of 1789 had a momentous impact on neighboring countries. The French Revolutionary
2:32:1552:14:14:72:540:574:12:Times:12:Times:armies during the 1790s and later under Napoleon invaded and controlled large parts of Europe. Together
2:33:1655:14:14:72:540:560:12:Times:12:Times:with invasion came various radical institutional changes. French invasion removed the legal and economic
2:34:1759:14:14:72:540:546:12:Times:12:Times:barriers that had protected the nobility, clergy, guilds, and urban oligarchies and established the principle
2:35:1868:14:14:72:540:532:12:Times:12:Times:of equality before the law. The evidence suggests that areas that were occupied by the French and
2:36:1965:14:14:72:540:518:12:Times:12:Times:that underwent radical institutional reform experienced more rapid urbanization and economic growth,
2:37:2065:14:14:72:540:504:12:Times:12:Times:especially after 1850. There is no evidence of a negative effect of French invasion. Our interpretation
2:38:2168:14:14:72:540:490:12:Times:12:Times:is that the Revolution destroyed (the institutional underpinnings of) the power of oligarchies and elites
2:39:2273:14:14:72:540:476:12:Times:12:Times:opposed to economic change; combined with the arrival of new economic and industrial opportunities
2:40:2371:14:14:72:540:462:12:Times:12:Times:in the second half of the 19th century, this helped pave the way for future economic growth. The evidence
2:41:2476:14:14:72:540:448:12:Times:12:Times:does not provide any support for several other views, most notably, that evolved institutions are inherently
2:42:2584:14:14:72:540:434:12:Times:12:Times:superior to those 'designed'; that institutions must be 'appropriate' and cannot be 'transplanted'; and
2:43:2687:14:42:72:451:420:12:Times:12:Times:that the civil code and other French institutions have adverse economic effects.
2:44:2767:42:14:72:155:378:12:Times:12:Times:Daron Acemoglu
2:45:2781:14:14:72:198:364:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Economics
2:46:2804:14:14:72:149:350:12:Times:12:Times:MIT, E52-380B
2:47:2817:14:14:72:165:336:12:Times:12:Times:50 Memorial Drive
2:48:2834:14:14:72:215:322:12:Times:12:Times:Cambridge, MA  02142-1347
2:49:2859:14:14:72:124:308:12:Times:12:Times:and NBER
2:50:2867:14:28:72:147:294:12:Times:12:Times:daron@mit.edu
2:51:2880:28:14:72:148:266:12:Times:12:Times:Davide Cantoni
2:52:2894:14:14:72:165:252:12:Times:12:Times:Harvard University

2:67:3142:14:14:306:439:224:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Government
2:68:3166:14:14:306:450:210:12:Times:12:Times:N309, 1737 Cambridge Street
2:69:3193:14:14:306:421:196:12:Times:12:Times:Cambridge, MA  02138
2:70:3213:14:14:306:358:182:12:Times:12:Times:and NBER
2:71:3221:14:0:306:440:168:12:Times:12:Times:jrobinson@gov.harvard.edu
3:72:3246:0:29:79:192:671:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:1    Introduction
3:73:3263:29:19:79:525:642:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The recent literature on comparative development has emphasized that underdevelopment is
3:74:3351:19:18:79:525:623:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:caused by institutions that do not create the right incentives for economic growth.  But there
3:75:3445:18:19:79:525:605:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is  disagreement  about  which  specific  institutions  are  important.   Some  scholars  emphasize
3:76:3544:19:18:79:525:586:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:property rights (North and Thomas, 1973, Acemoglu, Johnson and Robinson, 2001, Acemoglu
3:77:3631:18:18:79:525:568:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and Johnson, 2005), some legal institutions (La Porta, Lopez-de-Silanes, Shleifer, and Vishny,
3:78:3725:18:19:79:461:550:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1998) and others barriers to entry or oligarchies (Olson, 1982, Acemoglu, 2008).
3:79:3805:19:18:96:525:531:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Another unresolved issue is whether institutions can be designed,  and relatedly whether
3:80:3893:18:19:79:525:513:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:they can be reformed externally.  Hayek (1960) argued that institutions cannot be designed
3:81:3983:19:18:79:525:494:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and have to evolve organically (and that this was the major reason for the inferiority of the
3:82:4076:18:19:79:525:476:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:civil code), and a recent literature has claimed that institutions have to be ‘appropriate’ to the
3:83:4174:19:18:79:524:457:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:specific circumstances of countries (Berkowitz, Pistor and Richard, 2003a,b, Rodrik, 2007).1
3:84:4266:18:18:79:525:439:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:These problems are amplified when such reforms are implemented ‘Big Bang’ style.  Propo-
3:85:4354:18:19:79:525:421:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:nents of these views argue that enforced institutional change is likely to reduce prosperity, a
3:86:4449:19:18:79:525:402:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:claim that receives some support from the apparent failures of institutional reforms in Latin
3:87:4542:18:19:79:525:384:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:America, Africa, the former Soviet Union in the 1980s and 1990s, and the recent experiences in
3:88:4636:19:18:79:525:365:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Afghanistan and Iraq.  Not all external reform are failures, however, as evidenced, for example,
3:89:4732:18:18:79:416:347:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the successful US-imposed reforms in postwar Germany and Japan.
3:90:4798:18:19:96:525:329:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In this paper we exploit the variation in institutional reform created in Europe by the French
3:91:4892:19:18:79:525:310:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Revolution to investigate the consequences of radical, externally-imposed reform on subsequent
3:92:4986:18:19:79:525:292:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:economic growth.  After 1792 French armies invaded and reformed the institutions of many
3:93:5074:19:18:79:525:273:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:European countries.  The lessons from this episode are central to some of the current debates on
3:94:5170:18:19:79:525:255:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:institutions. First, the package of reforms the French imposed on areas they conquered included
3:95:5265:19:18:79:525:236:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the civil code, the abolition of guilds and the remnants of feudalism, and the introduction of
3:96:5359:18:18:79:525:218:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:equality before the law and the undermining of aristocratic privilege. These reforms thus clearly
3:97:5456:18:19:79:525:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:relate to the above-mentioned debates.  If oligarchies were the main barrier to economic growth
3:98:5551:19:18:79:525:181:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in Europe at the turn of the 19th century, then we would expect the Revolutionary reforms
3:99:5640:18:19:79:525:163:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to unleash more rapid economic growth in affected areas.  If, on the other hand, externally-
3:100:5732:19:16:79:525:144:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:imposed  radical  reform  is  generally  costly  or  if  the  civil  code  creates  major  distortions,  the
3:101:5840:16:14:91:525:128:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:1Relatedly, a dominant paradigm in economics maintains that institutions efficiently—albeit slowly—adapt
3:102:5944:14:11:79:525:114:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:to the underlying characteristics of society (e.g., Demsetz, 1967, Djankov, Glaeser, La Porta, Lopez-de-Silanes
3:103:6055:11:0:79:157:103:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:and Shleifer, 2003).
4:104:6076:0:18:79:257:671:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:reforms should have negative effects.2
4:105:6114:18:19:96:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We investigate the economic consequences of the French Revolution, and particularly, the
4:106:6202:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reforms that it imposed on the rest of Europe, by using two sources of historical data.  The first
4:107:6300:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is cross-national.  French armies invaded and reformed some parts of Europe but not others,
4:108:6391:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:so we can take those invaded as the ‘treatment group’ and compare their relative economic
4:109:6480:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:success before and after the revolutionary period.  Nevertheless, there is a substantial amount
4:110:6575:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of heterogeneity in this sample.  For instance, the French tended to have a greater impact on
4:111:6668:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:countries in Western Europe compared to Eastern Europe and even though we show that there
4:112:6757:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is no evidence of pre-trends, this does not rule out potential unobserved differences between
4:113:6850:19:18:79:241:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the treatment and control groups.
4:114:6883:18:19:96:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In addition to cross-national variation, we also examine variation within Germany.  Parts
4:115:6972:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of Germany,  primarily the west and northwest,  were invaded and reformed,  while the south
4:116:7063:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and the east were not.  Therefore we can construct more comparable treatment and control
4:117:7151:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:groups within Germany.  In addition, we collected data to develop several series of institutional
4:118:7248:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reforms  across  German  polities.   This  enables  us  both  to  verify  that  the  French  did  indeed
4:119:7352:18:19:79:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reform various aspects of institutions and to utilize a two-stage least squares strategy,  with
4:120:7447:19:18:79:353:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:French invasion as an instrument for institutional reform.
4:121:7505:18:19:96:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Crucially for our identification strategy, European countries or parts of Germany did not
4:122:7594:19:18:79:525:339:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f2:choosethe French institutions, but those institutions wereimposedon them first by the Revo-
4:123:7685:18:19:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lution and then by Napoleon.3Moreover, territorial expansion by French armies did not target
4:124:7777:19:18:79:525:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:places with a greater future growth potential.  Instead, it had two major objectives.  The first
4:125:7873:18:18:79:525:284:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:was defensive, especially, in response to the threat of Austrian or Prussian (or later British)
4:126:7968:18:19:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:attempts to topple the Revolutionary regime.  The second was expansionary.  This was partly
4:127:8059:19:18:79:525:247:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because of resource needs of the French Republic, and partly because of the ideology of the
4:128:8150:18:19:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:French Revolution.  In addition,  in the early 1790s,  the French sought to establish France’s
4:129:8244:19:16:79:525:210:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:‘natural frontiers.’4Finally, the purpose of the institutional reforms of the French Revolution
4:130:8339:16:14:91:525:194:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:2These issues are also related to the classic historical debate about the extent to which the institutions of
4:131:8448:14:11:79:525:180:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:theancien r ́egimeimpeded capitalism and economic growth and whether or not the French Revolution played a
4:132:8554:11:11:79:525:169:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:constructive or destructive role in European political development. The historical debate about the consequences
4:133:8666:11:11:79:525:158:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:of the French Revolution is also about its impact on political institutions and democracy, which is beyond the
4:134:8776:11:7:79:188:147:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:scope of the current paper.
4:135:8803:7:15:91:525:140:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:3In most cases, there were local Jacobin (local radical) forces in the countries invaded by the French armies,
4:136:8913:15:11:79:525:125:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:but the presence of such forces did not play a major role in determining which countres and cities were invaded
4:137:9024:11:7:79:313:114:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:by the French.  See, for example, Doyle (1989, Chapter 9).
4:138:9082:7:15:91:525:107:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:4For  example,  the  Revolutionary  leader  George  Danton  stated:  “Les  limites  de  la  France  sont  marqu ́ees
4:139:9198:15:0:79:525:92:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:par  la  nature,  nous  les  atteindrons  des  quatre  coins  de  l’horizon,  du  cˆot ́e  du  Rhin,  du  cˆot ́e  de  l’Oc ́ean,  du
5:140:9332:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:was  not  to  foster  industrialization  per  se,  though  they  may  have  achieved  this  objective  as
5:141:9437:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a  by-product  of  its  major  goal  of  destroying  the  grip  of  the  aristocracy,  oligarchy,  and  the
5:142:9544:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:clergy on political and economic power.5Therefore, to a first approximation, we can think of
5:143:9636:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the imposition of the institutions of the French Revolution as an ‘exogenous treatment’ and
5:144:9727:19:18:79:402:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:investigate the economic implications of the radical French reforms.
5:145:9795:18:19:96:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We  distinguish  three  definitions  of  ‘French  treatment:’  (1)  length  of  French  occupation
5:146:9893:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(in  years),  (2)  a  dummy  for  French  control  during  the  Revolutionary  period  prior  to  the
5:147:9994:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:take-over of Napoleon in 9 November 1799 (18 Brumaire in the revolutionary calendar), (3)
5:148:10083:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a  dummy  for  French  control  during  the  Napoleonic  period  up  until  1815.   Using  all  three
5:149:10184:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:definitions,  we find reduced-form evidence,  both across countries and within Germany,  that
5:150:10277:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:our main proxy for economic prosperity,  urbanization rates,  increased significantly faster in
5:151:10372:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:treated areas during the second half of the 19th century.  We also supplement our cross-country
5:152:10467:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analysis with Maddison’s GDP data.  Maddison reports data for ‘Germany’ and ‘Italy’ prior
5:153:10556:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to their unification,  rather than the independent polities which subsequently unified.  Much
5:154:10649:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of  the  variation  in  French  treatment,  however,  is  within  what  became  Germany  and  Italy,
5:155:10749:18:19:79:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:so Maddison’s data are much less appropriate than the urbanization data for our purposes.
5:156:10838:19:18:79:525:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Nevertheless, the findings with GDP per-capita are similar, though on the whole weaker than
5:157:10929:18:19:79:226:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:those using urbanization rates.
5:158:10960:19:18:96:525:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Within  Germany,  we  provide  supporting  evidence  using  the  expansion  of  railways  and
5:159:11053:18:19:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the sectoral composition of employment, again suggesting that treated parts of Germany grew
5:160:11144:19:18:79:525:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:more rapidly—in fact, industrialized more rapidly—after 1850.  Using German data, we further
5:161:11236:18:18:79:525:284:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:show a strong association between our measures of institutional reforms and French invasion
5:162:11327:18:19:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:or  control.   Using  this  association  as  a  first  stage,  we  also  estimate  instrumental-variables
5:163:11432:19:18:79:525:247:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:models,  which indicate large effects of institutional reforms on subsequent growth.  Overall,
5:164:11526:18:19:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:our results show no evidence that the reforms imposed by the French had negative economic
5:165:11615:19:19:79:525:210:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:consequences.  On the contrary, there is fairly consistent evidence from a variety of different
5:166:11710:19:11:79:525:191:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:cˆot ́e des Alpes.  L`a, doivent finir les bornes de notre r ́epublique.” (speech to National Convention, January 31,
5:167:11827:11:11:79:525:180:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:1793; quoted in Blanning 1983, p.  2).  Grab (2003, p.  1) summarizes these motives and arguments as:  “The
5:168:11934:11:11:79:525:169:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:revolutionary governments justified the occupation of foreign lands, using the theory of ‘natural frontiers’ and
5:169:12046:11:7:79:406:158:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:declaring their intention or liberating oppressed people from tyrannical regimes.”
5:170:12128:7:15:91:525:151:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:5It is unlikely that the reforms were made specifically to encourage industrial growth.  Most likely, no one
5:171:12236:15:11:79:525:136:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:at the turn of the 19th century could have anticipated the new technologies that were to arrive a few decades
5:172:12345:11:11:79:525:125:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:later.  The exception to this statement is textiles.  By 1800 the British and others had established some new
5:173:12454:11:11:79:525:114:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:technologies that increased productivity (e.g., in spinning) by an order of magnitude.  Textiles are an important
5:174:12567:11:11:79:525:103:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:part of the story in the Rhineland, discussed below, but there is no evidence that the French changed institutions
5:175:12681:11:0:79:460:92:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:in the Rhineland specifically because they foresaw great potential in the manufacture of cloth.
6:176:12777:0:18:79:317:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:empirical strategies that they had positive effects.
6:177:12829:18:19:96:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:An important aspect of our findings is that the positive effects of institutional reforms are
6:178:12922:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:only visible in the second half of the 19th century.  By 1850, treated areas show no differential
6:179:13019:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:growth or in some specifications, slight (and insignificant) negative growth.  This is not sur-
6:180:13114:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:prising.  The French reforms were accompanied by the disruptions caused by invasion and war
6:181:13205:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and this often had quite destructive and exploitative aspects (see, for instance, Blanning, 1983,
6:182:13302:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1986).  Grab (2003, p.  1), for example, writes “the French armies requisitioned provisions and
6:183:13397:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:imposed heavy war contributions on occupied regions, thereby alienating their populations.”
6:184:13488:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Thus,  the  short-term  impact  of  French  invasion  may  have  been  negative.   But  this  is  unin-
6:185:13591:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:formative about the impact of Revolution-imposed institutional changes.  The most plausible
6:186:13682:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:hypothesis—and our starting point—is that the major role of the reforms was in creating an
6:187:13772:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:environment conducive to innovation and entrepreneurial activity.  This environment mattered
6:188:13864:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:most in the dissemination of the industrial revolution, which took place in Continental Europe
6:189:13958:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in the second half of the 19th century.6Our evidence of positive effects in the second half of
6:190:14052:18:18:79:323:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the 19th century is consistent with this hypothesis.
6:191:14104:18:19:96:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:There are several main conclusions from the evidence presented in this paper.  First, the
6:192:14193:19:18:79:524:376:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:results emphasize the role of institutions and institutional reforms in economic development.7
6:193:14287:18:19:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f6:Second, our findings are consistent with the view that the institutions of theancien  r ́egime
6:194:14381:19:18:79:525:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(guilds,  feudal  legacy  in  countryside,  absence  of  equality  before  the  law)  impeded  growth.
6:195:14483:18:19:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:However, the fact that French reforms came as a bundle does not allow us to assess the rel-
6:196:14574:19:18:79:525:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ative importance of, for example, the abolition of guilds compared to the abolition of feudal
6:197:14667:18:18:79:525:284:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:privileges.  Third, they do not support the view that civil code and French institutions have
6:198:14760:18:16:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:detrimental  economic  effects.8Fourth,  the  evidence  does  not  support  the  thesis  that  insti-
6:199:14861:16:15:91:525:250:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:6This argument is similar to that of Engerman and Sokoloff (1997) and Acemoglu, Johnson and Robinson
6:200:14961:15:11:79:525:235:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:(2002) who argue that the divergence of institutions in colonial societies, which took place between 1500 and
6:201:15070:11:7:79:314:224:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:1800, had little economic impact until the age of industry.
6:202:15129:7:15:91:525:217:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:7Also noteworthy is that unlike the experience of European colonialism in the wider world, the French did
6:203:15234:15:11:79:525:202:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:not settle after 1815 in the parts of Europe they reformed and they did not build schools, so during this episode
6:204:15347:11:11:79:525:191:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:there were large changes in institutions with culture, human capital and the ethnic composition of the population
6:205:15460:11:7:79:197:180:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:remaining largely unchanged.
6:206:15488:7:15:91:525:173:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:8This statement is subject to two obvious caveats, firstly, that the comparison is not to the common law,
6:207:15593:15:11:79:525:158:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:and secondly,  that it could be that the imposition of the civil code did have negative effects, but these were
6:208:15704:11:11:79:525:147:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:more than compensated for by positive effects emanating from reforms simultaneously implemented with the
6:209:15808:11:11:79:525:136:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:civil code.  These caveats notwithstanding, if the civil code and other aspects of French institutions were highly
6:210:15922:11:11:79:525:125:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:damaging to growth,  particularly if imposed on other countries,  we would expect to find significant negative
6:211:16032:11:11:79:525:114:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:effects in treated areas.  In addition, apart from parts of the world that voluntarily adopted the civil code, such
6:212:16147:11:11:79:525:103:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:as Latin America, existing evidence on the consequences of the civil code comes from former French colonies
6:213:16254:11:0:79:495:92:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:which, like the Europe we study, had the civil code imposed simultaneously with other French reforms.
7:214:16356:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tutions are efficiently adapted to the underlying characteristics of a society and that evolved
7:215:16451:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:institutions are superior to those that are designed or externally imposed.  The French Revolu-
7:216:16546:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tionary armies imposed new and radically different institutions from those existing previously,
7:217:16641:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and did so in extreme ‘Big Bang’ style.  Nevertheless, our evidence suggests that this was an
7:218:16734:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:‘economic  success’.   Taken  together,  these  findings  are  interesting  for  those  who  favor  radi-
7:219:16839:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cal institutional reforms.  They suggest these may be successful, at least in certain historical
7:220:16935:19:18:79:121:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:contexts.
7:221:16944:18:18:96:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The  success  of  the  French  reforms  raises  the  question:   why  did  they  work  when  other
7:222:17042:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:externally-imposed reforms often fail?  Most likely this is because the reforms were much more
7:223:17136:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:radical than is typically the case.9The French reformed simultaneously in many dimensions
7:224:17225:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and weakened the powers of local elites, making a return to the status quo ante largely impossi-
7:225:17321:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ble.  Even when some pre-revolution elites returned to power after 1815, there was a permanent
7:226:17415:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:change in the political equilibrium.  This scope and radicalism of the French reforms are com-
7:227:17509:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:mon with the post-war reform experiences in Germany and Japan and stand in contrast with
7:228:17597:18:18:79:229:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:many other reform experiences.
7:229:17627:18:19:96:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In addition to the literature on the implications and desirability of different types of insti-
7:230:17722:19:18:79:525:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tutional reforms, discussed above, our paper is related to a large literature on the consequences
7:231:17819:18:19:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the French Revolution.  The debate on the French Revolution was started in its modern form
7:232:17912:19:18:79:525:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the pamphlet published by the conservative English philosopher Edmund Burke in 1790,
7:233:17999:18:19:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:entitledReflections  on  the  Revolution  in  France,which initiated controversies about institu-
7:234:18096:19:18:79:525:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tional  change  which  continue  today.   In  this  pamphlet,  Burke  condemned  the  brutality,  the
7:235:18197:18:26:79:451:284:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:interventionist spirit and the radicalism of the French Revolution and argued:
7:236:18275:26:19:122:497:258:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“It is with infinite caution that any man should venture upon pulling down an
7:237:18352:19:18:106:498:239:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:edifice, which has answered in any tolerable degree for ages the common purposes of
7:238:18435:18:18:106:498:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:society, or on building it up again without having models and patterns of approved
7:239:18517:18:27:106:315:203:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:utility before his eyes” Burke (1969, p.152).
7:240:18562:27:18:79:525:176:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The  conclusion  Burke  drew  from  these  events  was  that  the  negative  impacts  of  the  French
7:241:18663:18:18:79:525:158:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Revolution  would  be  felt  not  only  in  France  and  not  only  in  its  immediate  aftermath,  but
7:242:18766:18:19:79:489:140:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:would potentially change the world for many more decades or even centuries to come.
7:243:18849:19:18:96:525:121:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:On the other side, the positive reception of the French Revolution was as enthusiastic as
7:244:18938:18:15:79:525:103:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Burke’s condemnation.  Thomas Paine, in a book that would subsequently become a classic for
7:245:19029:15:0:91:497:88:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:9See Acemoglu and Robinson (2008) for a model in which limited reforms can be counterproductive.
8:246:19126:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the democratization movements of the 19th-century Europe,The Rights of Man, responded to
8:247:19214:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Burke in 1791.  Paine hailed the French Revolution as the harbinger of freedom and equality
8:248:19305:19:30:79:496:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:before the law, a role that it achieved by demolishing theancien r ́egime.  Paine argued:
8:249:19394:30:19:122:497:604:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“It was . . .  against the despotic principles of the government, that the nation
8:250:19475:19:18:106:498:585:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:revolted.  These principles had . . .  become too deeply rooted to be removed, and
8:251:19557:18:19:106:498:567:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Augean stable of parasites and plunderers too abominably filthy to be cleansed,
8:252:19640:19:30:106:482:548:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by anything short of a complete and universal revolution” Paine (1969, p.  69).
8:253:19719:30:18:79:525:518:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:According to Paine, the French Revolution was exactly the kind of radical institutional reform
8:254:19813:18:19:79:525:500:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:necessary  to  break  the  hold  on  land  and  people  exercised  by  theancien  r ́egime,  which  was
8:255:19916:19:18:79:525:481:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:not  only  morally  abhorrent,  but  also  the  source  of  significant  economic  inefficiencies.   The
8:256:20020:18:19:79:525:463:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Revolution would therefore pave the way for modern freedoms and democratic institutions by
8:257:20110:19:18:79:525:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:removing serfdom, aristocratic privileges, the Church’s domination over politics and land, and
8:258:20204:18:18:79:192:426:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:inequity before the law.
8:259:20228:18:19:96:525:408:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The debate between Burke and Paine has not been resolved by academic research and there
8:260:20315:19:18:79:525:389:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is no consensus about the economic consequences of the French Revolution.  On the one hand,
8:261:20406:18:19:79:525:371:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:many economic historians, like Landes (1969, p.  142), view the French Revolution as “a po-
8:262:20497:19:18:79:525:352:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:litical roadblock” to technological adoption for Continental countries, and conclude that as a
8:263:20591:18:19:79:525:334:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:consequence of the Revolution, “the gap in technique [between the Continent and Britain] had
8:264:20683:19:18:79:525:315:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:widened, while most of the fundamental educational, economic, and social obstacles to imita-
8:265:20775:18:18:79:525:297:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tion remained” (Landes, 1969, p.  147).  Similarly, Buyst and Mokyr (1990, p.  64, 74) write: “it
8:266:20872:18:19:79:525:279:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is our contention . . .  that the Dutch economy in the years of the French and Napoleonic Wars
8:267:20966:19:18:79:525:260:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:was another example of a small open commercial economy whose prosperity was disrupted by
8:268:21054:18:19:79:525:242:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:world events . . .  The French period . . .  [was] disastrous for the Dutch economy.” Crouzet’s
8:269:21149:19:18:79:525:223:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2001, p.  121) view is similar, noting “the French Revolution and the wars that followed greatly
8:270:21246:18:16:79:251:205:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:slowed the transfer of technology.”10
8:271:21283:16:15:87:525:189:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:10Within the history profession the economic impact of the French Revolution has become inexorably inter-
8:272:21388:15:11:79:525:174:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:twined with the Marxist interpretation of the revolution as marking the ‘rise of the bourgoisie’.  Most historians
8:273:21502:11:11:79:525:163:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:now reject a Marxist interpretation of the Revolution, but have not articulated an alternative assessment of its
8:274:21614:11:11:79:525:152:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:implications.  Other economic historians simply ignore the Revolution.  In the literature on 19th century German
8:275:21726:11:11:79:525:141:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:industrialization, for example, though it is common to argue that the Stein-Hardenberg Reforms in Prussia after
8:276:21837:11:11:79:525:130:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:1807, which were induced by the defeat of Prussia by Napoleon at Jena, helped to modernize agriculture and
8:277:21943:11:11:79:525:119:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:facilitate the reallocation of labor to industrial areas (e.g., Tilly, 1996), little mention is made of any potential
8:278:22060:11:11:79:525:108:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:impact of French institutional reforms in Germany.  The institutional reform most mentioned is the formation
8:279:22168:11:0:79:241:97:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:of the Zollverein customs union in 1833.
9:280:22209:0:18:96:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:On the other hand, many other economic historians also agree with Mokyr’s assessments
9:281:22294:18:19:79:526:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that  “the  Revolution’s  long-term  effect  was  to  clear  up  the  debris  of  theancien  r ́egimeon
9:282:22397:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Continent, thus assuring Europe’s ability eventually to follow Britain in revolutionizing its
9:283:22494:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:productive system” (1990, p.  259), and that   “the French Revolution and Napoleon installed
9:284:22586:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:more forward looking governments in Europe” (1990, p.  253). Even Landes, who generally em-
9:285:22677:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:phasizes the negative effects of the Revolution, also mentions in passing that such institutional
9:286:22774:19:18:79:419:560:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:changes as the abolition of guilds were beneficial (1969, pp.  144-145).11
9:287:22848:18:18:96:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The rest of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 provides an overview of the history of
9:288:22943:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the French Revolution and the subsequent invasion of Europe by the French. Section 3 discusses
9:289:23037:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:our data.  Section 4 provides country-level regression evidence investigating the reduced form
9:290:23131:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:effect of treatment on our dependent variables.  Section 5 looks at the differences in the growth
9:291:23228:19:34:79:433:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:experience of treated and untreated German polities.  Section 6 concludes.
9:292:23302:34:29:79:437:434:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:2    The Effect of the French Revolution on Europe
9:293:23352:29:19:79:525:405:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In this section, we provide a brief sketch of certain aspects of the French Revolution and the
9:294:23446:19:18:79:525:386:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:situation in various neighboring countries and cities before the Revolution that are relevant
9:295:23539:18:19:79:525:368:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to our investigation.  We also recount how expansion by the French Revolutionary armies and
9:296:23630:19:18:79:526:349:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:later by Napoleon affected these areas.12Further historical details are provided in Acemoglu,
9:297:23723:18:18:79:269:331:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Cantoni, Johnson and Robinson (2009).
9:298:23760:18:19:96:525:313:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The Revolution was precipitated by a long-running fiscal crisis which led to the convening
9:299:23850:19:18:79:525:294:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f6:of theEstates-Generalin 1789 for the first time since 1614.  The initial meeting of theEstates-
9:300:23945:18:19:79:525:276:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f2:Generalin  Versailles  on  May  5,  1789  ended  with  the  decision  to  convene  a  more  powerful
9:301:24045:19:18:79:525:257:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:body,  theNational  Assembly,  which  initiated  a  process  of  radicalization  culminating  in  the
9:302:24146:18:19:79:526:239:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f6:storming of the Bastille on July 14, 1789.  The newly-formedNational  Constituent  Assembly
9:303:24237:19:18:79:525:220:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f6:abolished feudalism and all of the special privileges and rights of theFirstandSecond Estates
9:304:24330:18:18:79:525:202:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on August 4, 1789.  This was followed by the abolition of the Church’s authority to levy special
9:305:24426:18:19:79:525:184:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:taxes, and later turning the clergy into employees of the state, thus starting the process of the
9:306:24523:19:16:79:525:165:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:separation of church and state.  It subsequently passed a constitution on September 29, 1791,
9:307:24616:16:15:87:525:149:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:11See  also  Crouzet  (2001,  p.   122),  Cameron  (1993,  pp.   211-213)  and  Rosenthal  (1992).   Similarly,  Rud ́e
9:308:24735:15:11:79:525:134:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:(1964), Kisch (1962), Trebilcock (1981), Doyle (1989), or Grab (2003) also argue that the French reforms were
9:309:24844:11:10:79:525:123:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:significant improvements relative to the situation at the time.  None of these works systematically investigates
9:310:24956:10:8:79:403:113:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:the economic consequences of the Revolution either in the short or the long run.
9:311:25036:8:14:87:525:105:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:12This section draws on Doyle (1989), Palmer (1959, 1964), Rud ́e (1988), Grab (2003), and Blanning (1983,
9:312:25142:14:0:79:128:91:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:1986, 1996).
10:313:25155:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:making France a constitutional monarchy.  This constitution also removed the major power of
10:314:25246:18:19:79:188:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the guilds in the cities.
10:315:25271:19:18:96:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The radicalization of the Revolution led to theTerrorwhich ended with the execution of
10:316:25357:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Robespierre and Saint-Just in July 1794.  There followed a phase of relative stability, first in
10:317:25453:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the form of collective government under the Directory between 1795 and 1799, and then with
10:318:25543:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:more concentrated power in the form of a three person Consulate, consisting of Ducos, Sieyes
10:319:25635:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and  Napoleon  Bonaparte.   Already  during  the  directory,  the  young  general  Bonaparte  had
10:320:25732:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:become famous for his military successes and statesmanship.  His influence was only to grow
10:321:25823:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:after 1799.  The Consulate soon became personal rule by Napoleon, who initially engineered
10:322:25913:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:his election as First Consul in November 1799 with the coup of the 18th of Brumaire and then
10:323:26005:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:declared himself emperor in 1804.  The years between 1799 and the end of Napoleon’s reign,
10:324:26095:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1815, witnessed a series of great victories, including those at Austerlitz, Jena-Auerstedt, and
10:325:26190:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Wagram, bringing continental Europe to its knees.  They also allowed Napoleon to impose his
10:326:26281:19:29:79:350:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:will (and his legal code) across a wide swath of territory.
10:327:26340:29:25:79:287:402:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.1    Europe Before the Revolution
10:328:26375:25:19:79:525:377:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Before the age of the French Revolution, much of Europe was dominated by two kinds of oli-
10:329:26465:19:18:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:garchies, the landed nobility in agriculture and the urban-based oligarchy controlling commerce
10:330:26560:18:19:79:385:340:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and various occupations, with explicit or implicit entry barriers.
10:331:26626:19:18:96:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:By  the  end  of  the  eighteenth  century,  feudalism  in  its  most  rigid  form  had  disappeared
10:332:26726:18:18:79:525:303:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in many parts of Europe, but a lot of its remnants remained.  Serfdom—the system through
10:333:26814:18:19:79:525:285:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:which peasants are tied to the land and cannot sell their labor in free markets or engage in
10:334:26906:19:18:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:other occupations without the permission of landowners—still continued in much of Eastern
10:335:26995:18:19:79:525:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Europe (see Blum, 1978), while it had been replaced by various forms of taxes and tributes to
10:336:27088:19:18:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:landowners in other areas, which could nonetheless be quite onerous.13For example, in the
10:337:27177:18:18:79:525:211:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Rhineland,  the  first  area  in  Germany  to  come  under  French  control,  an  attenuated  form  of
10:338:27279:18:19:79:525:193:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:serfdom (Grundherrschaft) which severely restricted freedom of movement was still practiced
10:339:27370:19:18:79:525:174:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(Blanning 1983, pp.  20-21).  Grab (2003, p.  86) states:  “Their conditions were worse east of
10:340:27465:18:19:79:525:156:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Elbe where serfdom still prevailed.  But even in many western regions where serfdom had
10:341:27556:19:16:79:525:137:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:declined and peasants were freer and better off, they were often still subject to landlords to
10:342:27650:16:14:87:525:121:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:13Since one could be concerned that including Eastern Europe in the sample leads the control group to be
10:343:27754:14:11:79:525:107:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:very  heterogeneous,  in  the  empirical  work  we  show  that  all  our  results  hold  when  we  restrict  our  sample  to
10:344:27878:11:0:79:181:96:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:Europe West of the Elbe.
11:345:27903:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:whom they owed seigneurial fees and labor obligations.  In addition they had to pay taxes and
11:346:27996:18:19:79:311:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:support their parishes and village communities.”
11:347:28044:19:18:96:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Even when serfdom in its classic form was absent, various rights of the nobility and clergy
11:348:28135:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:created  a  very  unequal  political  and  economic  situation  in  rural  areas.   These  groups  were
11:349:28238:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:frequently exempt from taxation by the state and enjoyed the right of taxation of the peasants
11:350:28332:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:under their control.  Lenger (2004, p.  92), for example, describes this as:  “besides the origi-
11:351:28429:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:nal obligations to provide services and dues to the lord the agricultural labor force was also
11:352:28523:18:26:79:316:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:burdened with personal servitude”.  He continues:
11:353:28572:26:19:106:498:516:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“In the small territory of Nassau-Usingen around 1800 there were no less than 230
11:354:28653:19:18:106:498:497:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:different payments, dues, and services that the peasants living there had to provide
11:355:28737:18:18:106:498:479:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to the lords.  Dues included . . .  the ‘blood tithe’ to be paid after an animal was
11:356:28821:18:19:106:498:461:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:slaughtered, a ‘bee tithe’, a ‘wax tithe’ . . .  as well as large fees owed to the lord
11:357:28908:19:26:106:433:442:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:whenever a piece of property changed hands.” (Lenger, 2004, p.  96).
11:358:28976:26:18:79:525:416:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Moreover, in places where some form of seigneurial privilege remained, it was usual for nobility
11:359:29072:18:19:79:525:398:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and clergy to be subject to different laws and courts.  The principle of equality before the law
11:360:29168:19:18:79:395:379:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:was quite alien (or even revolutionary) in most of Europe in 1789.
11:361:29234:18:19:96:525:361:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The  urban  oligarchy  was  perhaps  even  more  pernicious  to  industrialization.   Almost  all
11:362:29331:19:18:79:525:342:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:major occupations were controlled by guilds, significantly limiting entry into those professions
11:363:29427:18:19:79:525:324:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by others, and often restricting adoption of new technologies (see White, 2001, for examples
11:364:29519:19:18:79:525:305:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from French guilds).  For example, Cipolla (1970) argues that the guilds stopped innovation
11:365:29610:18:18:79:525:287:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in Italy, in particular they forbade the production of exactly the type of lower quality goods
11:366:29704:18:19:79:525:269:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that were taking their markets.  In Venice (p.  206) “for almost the whole of the 17th century,
11:367:29799:19:18:79:525:250:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the  statutes  of  the  guild  prevented  cloth  from  being  made  of  the  English  and  Dutch  type,
11:368:29902:18:19:79:525:232:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:which had had so much success on the international markets.  Moreover, the guild statutes not
11:369:29995:19:18:79:525:213:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:only demanded the production of a traditional type of goods, but also prevented the adoption
11:370:30087:18:19:79:525:195:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of new methods of making these old products.” Braudel’s (1992, p.  136) and Rapp’s (1976)
11:371:30176:19:18:79:525:176:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analysis is similar.  For the  case of Germany,  a similar argument  is made by  Ogilvie  (2004,
11:372:30272:18:18:79:525:158:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:2007)  and  Kisch  (1989)  for  the  Rhineland.   In  the  major  cities  of  Cologne  and  Aachen  the
11:373:30375:18:19:79:525:140:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:adoption of new textile (spinning and weaving) machines were significantly delayed because
11:374:30465:19:18:79:525:121:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of  guild  restrictions.   In  addition,  many  cities  were  controlled  by  a  few  families  for  many
11:375:30570:18:19:79:525:103:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:generations, amassing wealth at the expense of potential new entrants with greater ability or
11:376:30663:19:0:79:172:84:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:better technologies.
12:377:30684:0:26:79:226:671:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.2    Under the Republic
12:378:30709:26:18:79:525:645:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Despite the fact that the French Revolution was immediately seen as threatening to Europe’s
12:379:30800:18:18:79:525:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:elite, war—the so-called War of the First Coalition—did not break out until 1792.14Contrary
12:380:30891:18:19:79:525:609:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to almost everyone’s expectations,  after some early defeats,  the armies of the new Republic
12:381:30984:19:18:79:525:590:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:were victorious in an initially defensive war.  There were serious organizational problems to
12:382:31077:18:19:79:525:572:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:overcome, but particularly after the introduction of mass conscription (thelev ́ee en masse) in
12:383:31172:19:18:79:451:553:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:August 1793, the French had a military advantage than verged on supremacy.
12:384:31246:18:18:96:525:535:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Initial military success encouraged the Republic’s leadership to expand France’s borders,
12:385:31335:18:19:79:525:517:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with  an  eye  towards  creating  an  effective  buffer  between  the  new  Republic  and  the  hostile
12:386:31438:19:18:79:525:498:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:monarchs  of  Prussia  and  Austria.   The  French  quickly  seized  present-day  Belgium  and  the
12:387:31537:18:19:79:525:480:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Netherlands (where ice famously allowed French cavalry to capture a large part of the Dutch
12:388:31628:19:18:79:525:461:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:navy).  The French also gained effective control over much of modern-day Switzerland.  In all
12:389:31721:18:19:79:377:443:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:three places, the French had strong control through the 1790s.
12:390:31783:19:18:96:525:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Germany was initially hotly contested but by 1795 the French had firm control over the
12:391:31869:18:18:79:525:406:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Rhineland (the west or left bank of the Rhine), and the Prussians were forced to recognize this
12:392:31964:18:19:79:525:388:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:fact under the Treaty of Basle.15Between 1795 and 1802, the French firmly held the Rhineland,
12:393:32057:19:18:79:525:369:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:but not any other part of Germany.  In 1802 the Rhineland was officially incorporated into
12:394:32147:18:19:79:114:351:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:France.
12:395:32154:19:18:96:525:332:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Italy remained the main seat of war in the second half the 1790s, with the Austrians as
12:396:32241:18:19:79:525:314:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the  main  opponents.   Savoy  was  annexed  by  France  in  1792,  and  a  stalemate  was  reached
12:397:32340:19:18:79:525:295:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:until Napoleon’s invasion in April 1796.  In his first major continental campaign, by early 1797
12:398:32436:18:18:79:525:277:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon had swept up almost all of Northern Italy, except for Venice (which was taken by
12:399:32525:18:19:79:525:259:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the  Austrians).   The  Treaty  of  Campo  Formio,  signed  with  the  Austrians  in  October  1797,
12:400:32625:19:18:79:525:240:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ended the War of the First Coalition and recognized a number of French-controlled republics
12:401:32716:18:19:79:163:222:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in Northern Italy.
12:402:32734:19:18:96:525:203:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:However,  the  French  continued  to  expand  their  control  over  Italy  even  after  this  treaty.
12:403:32835:18:19:79:525:185:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In March 1798 the French invaded the Papal States and established the Roman Republic.  In
12:404:32924:19:16:79:525:166:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:January  1799,  Naples  was  conquered  and  the  Parthenopean  Republic  created.16With  the
12:405:33017:16:14:87:525:150:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:14The  initial  reaction  in  European  capitals  was  amazement  but  there  was  no  perceived  need  to  intervene.
12:406:33135:14:11:79:525:136:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:This changed with the execution of Louis XVI. See Doyle (1989) for general background and Blanning (1986)
12:407:33240:11:11:79:525:125:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:for a more detailed treatment.  Esdaile (1995, 2001) and Ellis (2003) are succinct overviews of all of the wars
12:408:33351:11:7:79:407:114:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:from 1792 to 1815, while Blanning (1996) and Gates (1997) provide more details.
12:409:33430:7:11:87:420:107:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:15Part of the broader 1795 Peace of Basle with Prussia, Spain, and Hessen-Kassel.
12:410:33511:11:0:87:525:96:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:16The Parthenopean Republic was short-lived, but the French were soon back and—under Napoleon—seem to
13:411:33614:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:exception of Venice (still Austrian), the French now controlled the entire Italian peninsular ei-
13:412:33711:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ther directly (Savoy) or through satellite republics (Cisalpine, Ligurian, Roman, and Partheno-
13:413:33806:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:pean).  There was further back-and-forth in the War of the Second Coalition (1798-1801), but
13:414:33898:18:30:79:363:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:this ended with the French essentially remaining in control.
13:415:33958:30:25:79:464:586:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.3    Institutional Changes Induced by the Revolutionary Armies
13:416:34022:25:18:79:525:561:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Many  of  the  most  radical  institutional  changes  were  undertaken  during  the  invasion  of  the
13:417:34124:18:19:79:525:543:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:French  Revolutionary  armies.   This  included  the  abolition  of  all  the  remaining  vestiges  of
13:418:34226:19:18:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:serfdom  and  quasi-feudal  land  relations,  the  hold  of  the  clergy  over  economic  and  political
13:419:34330:18:19:79:525:506:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:power,  the  domination  of  the  guilds  in  urban  areas,  and  legal  changes  establishing  equality
13:420:34434:19:18:79:150:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:before the law.
13:421:34449:18:19:96:525:469:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Belgium “The clergy and the nobility, the two upper estates, enjoyed many privileges
13:422:34536:19:18:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and possessed considerable wealth and political power . . .  Urban guilds and corporations, the
13:423:34631:18:18:79:525:432:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:dominant element in the Third Estate, also possessed important privileges and controlled the
13:424:34723:18:19:79:525:414:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:towns” (Grab, 2003, p.  75).  France annexed Belgium in 1795 and ruled for 20 years.  Equality
13:425:34817:19:18:79:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:before the law was established and seigneurial rights, the tithe, noble privileges, and the guilds
13:426:34915:18:19:79:525:377:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:were abolished; in fact, from December 1796, any new French legislation became applicable in
13:427:35007:19:18:79:218:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Belgium (Grab, 2003, p.  78).
13:428:35036:18:19:96:525:340:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In the Netherlands (or United Provinces), the existing institutions were more those of en-
13:429:35126:19:18:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:trenched urban oligarchies in the various cities.  These oligarchies were effectively expropriated
13:430:35224:18:18:79:525:303:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the French and, after considerable upheaval, the Batavian Republic was established along
13:431:35315:18:19:79:258:285:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:French lines (Grab, 2003, Chapter 4).
13:432:35352:19:18:96:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Switzerland, the situation was similar to the Netherlands.  Before 1789, “Feudal priv-
13:433:35441:18:19:79:525:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ileges persisted in many rural communities.  Peasants owed the Church and landlords tithe,
13:434:35531:19:18:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:seigneurial fees, and labor services.  Many Swiss lacked the freedom to settle where they wanted
13:435:35627:18:18:79:525:211:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to or choose the occupation they desired.” (Grab,  2003,  p.  113).  With the coming of Rev-
13:436:35719:18:19:79:525:193:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:olutionary  armies,  existing  oligarchs  were  dispossessed  or  forced  to  cede  power,  and  feudal
13:437:35822:19:18:79:223:174:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:privileges largely disappeared.
13:438:35853:18:19:96:525:156:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Germany, the only real changes during this period were in the Rhineland (West Bank
13:439:35938:19:18:79:525:137:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the Rhine).  While the impact of the French on the Rhineland during the 1790s remains
13:440:36026:18:20:79:525:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:controversial, especially because of the great deal of plunder and the resulting resentment by
13:441:36120:20:11:79:525:99:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:have had a great impact on some institutions in the south of Italy, on which see Davis (1991, 2003) or Gregory
13:442:36230:11:0:79:107:88:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:(2001).
14:443:36239:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the local population mentioned above (see, e.g., Blanning, 1983, Doyle, 1989), the importance
14:444:36332:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the revolutionary reforms in Rhineland is not in question.  Even a critic of the French, such
14:445:36428:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:as Blanning (1983), admits the significant institutional reforms undertaken by the French in
14:446:36520:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Rhineland.  Most significantly, in 1798 the seigneurial regime and the guilds were abolished
14:447:36616:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(Blanning, 1983, pp.  137 and 155), and this paved the way to a relatively free labor market.
14:448:36709:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:This was a major reform and meant that master artisans could (and did) set up their shops
14:449:36798:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:when  they  wished  to  do  so  (Diefendorf  1980,  p.   164).   Equally  important  were  the  legal
14:450:36899:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:changes.  For example, the French created a commercial court in Aachen in 1794, and followed
14:451:36991:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with similar courts elsewhere in the Rhineland (Diefendorf, 1980, pp.  159-160), which were to
14:452:37085:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:play an important role in the creation of commercial and industrial businesses in the years to
14:453:37179:18:19:79:110:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:follow.
14:454:37186:19:18:96:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Italy, several new Republics were established with French-style constitutions, parliaments
14:455:37279:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(on a property-based franchise), and more efficient systems of administration.  Extensive aris-
14:456:37374:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tocratic privileges were abolished and the notion of equality before the law was introduced in
14:457:37468:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnest for the first time (see Doyle, 1989, Chapter 15, Grab, 2003, Chapter 10, Broers, 1997,
14:458:37562:18:30:79:108:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:2005).
14:459:37568:30:25:79:244:365:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.4    Napoleon At the Helm
14:460:37595:25:19:79:525:340:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:After Napoleon’s takeover, the French impact spread much wider throughout Europe.  In Ger-
14:461:37685:19:18:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:many, where the direct control of the Revolutionary armies had been limited to the Rhineland,
14:462:37778:18:18:79:525:303:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon constructed a string of satellite buffer states on France’s northeastern border.  There
14:463:37874:18:19:79:525:285:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:were  several  iterations,  but  the  big  break  point  followed  the  Peace  of  Lun ́eville  (February
14:464:37979:19:18:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1801), with a massive reorganization of the territories that comprised the Holy Roman Empire
14:465:38071:18:19:79:525:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(roughly Austria and Germany in their 1914 borders).  Much of the astonishing variety of the
14:466:38163:19:18:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Empire disappeared—literally hundreds of independent states, ecclesiastical territories and free
14:467:38259:18:18:79:525:211:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:imperial cities vanished and were consolidated into a cluster of larger kingdoms, principalities,
14:468:38356:18:19:79:525:193:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and duchies; ultimately, their number shrank to fewer than 40 states (Grab, 2003, pp.  89-90).
14:469:38450:19:18:79:525:174:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The main beneficiaries were the Grand Duchy of Baden and the kingdoms of W ̈urttenberg and
14:470:38540:18:19:79:525:156:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Bavaria (all in the South of Germany).  These and most other German states except Prussia
14:471:38629:19:18:79:525:137:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:were brought together in 1806 in theRheinbund, known in English as the Confederation of the
14:472:38720:18:19:79:206:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Rhine (see Schmitt, 1983).
14:473:38746:19:0:96:525:100:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:At the same time, Napoleon proceeded to reorganize the territories in northwest Germany
15:474:38835:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:into satellite states under his control.  The Duchy of Berg on the right bank of the Rhine was
15:475:38929:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:formed  in  March  1806  (it  was  supposed  to  be  ruled  by  his  infant  nephew  Louis  Napoleon),
15:476:39031:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Kingdom of Westphalia (ruled by his brother J ́erˆome) in August 1807, and the Duchy of
15:477:39122:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Frankfurt in February 1810 (it was planned that Napoleon’s stepson, Eugene de Beauharnais,
15:478:39212:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:would  inherit  it).   These  were  run  by  the  French  and  persisted  until  the  collapse  following
15:479:39317:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon’s invasion of Russia.  During this period Napoleon also took over parts of Northern
15:480:39409:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Germany,  including in December 1810 the annexation into France of Hamburg,  L ̈ubeck and
15:481:39498:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Bremen (Hanseatic cities) and the plains of Lower Saxony, which would later comprise most
15:482:39587:18:19:79:212:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the Kingdom of Hanover.
15:483:39613:19:18:96:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Elsewhere  the  French  maintained  their  control  over  the  Low  Countries,  and  in  1808  in-
15:484:39711:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:vaded Spain.  In Italy Napoleon re-created the Cisalpine (subsequently Italian) and Ligurian
15:485:39803:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Republics and created the satellite state of the Kingdom of Etruria comprising the larger part
15:486:39897:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of Tuscany.  By March 1802 the French commanded all of Italy except again the Papal States,
15:487:39988:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Naples  and  Venice.   Piedmont  was  annexed  to  France  in  early  1803,  and  in  1805  Napoleon
15:488:40088:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:turned the Italian Republic into the Kingdom of Italy (1805 to 1814) and the Ligurian Repub-
15:489:40180:18:19:79:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lic and Parma were made part of France.  In 1806, the French again invaded southern Italy and
15:490:40273:19:18:79:525:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon installed his brother Joseph as the King of Naples.  The Papal States were invaded
15:491:40364:18:19:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the French in 1808,  and most was annexed to France,  forming the departments of Tiber
15:492:40453:19:18:79:525:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and Trasimene.  In 1808, Joseph was made King of Spain, and the Kingdom of Naples passed
15:493:40541:18:29:79:298:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to Napoleon’s brother-in-law, Joachim Murat.
15:494:40585:29:26:79:228:292:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.5    Napoleon’s Reforms
15:495:40610:26:18:79:525:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Although Napoleon was an Emperor seeking to solidify his control, ruthlessly when necessary, in
15:496:40705:18:19:79:525:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:France and throughout the French Empire, he nonetheless continued to implement the reforms
15:497:40795:19:18:79:525:229:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:initiated by the Revolutionary armies (see Grab, 2003, Connolly, 1965, and Woolf, 1991).  As
15:498:40887:18:18:79:525:211:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Grab (2003, p.  xi) puts it:  “Revolutionary France had initiated many of these changes and
15:499:40978:18:19:79:525:193:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon exported them throughout Europe.” Napoleon himself saw the imposition of the civil
15:500:41069:19:18:79:525:174:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:code (Code  Napol ́eon) in the areas he controlled as his most important reform (Lyons, 1994,
15:501:41162:18:19:79:525:156:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:p.  94).  In practice Napoleon’s institutional legacy outside of France is complicated, especially
15:502:41260:19:18:79:525:137:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:since he was more inclined to compromise with local elites at some times, but in most places
15:503:41352:18:19:79:521:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:there was a genuine attempt to continue and deepen the reforms brought by the Revolution.
15:504:41441:19:0:96:525:100:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The motivation for the reforms seems to have been several.  First, Napoleon had himself
16:505:41530:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:been deeply involved with the reforms of the Revolutionary period and seems to have shared the
16:506:41624:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ideological commitment of the early reformers.  Second, like them, he wished to build a series
16:507:41718:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of buffer states around France.  Finally, reforms such as abolishing elites, feudal privileges and
16:508:41816:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:introducing equality before the law undermined existing elites and made it easier for Napoleon
16:509:41910:19:18:79:339:597:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:to establish control over the areas rule he conquered.17
16:510:41966:18:19:96:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Germany, Napoleon sustained the reforms already introduced into the Rhineland par-
16:511:42051:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ticularly introducing theCode  Napol ́eon.  Kisch emphasizes the economic importance of this
16:512:42143:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(1989,  p.   212):  “When  the  many  strands  of  commercial  legislation  were  subsequently  con-
16:513:42243:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:solidated in theCode  Napol ́eon, the Rhineland (on the left bank) was not only given a most
16:514:42335:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:up-to-date legal framework, but also a system of government in close harmony with the needs
16:515:42426:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of a buoyantly industrializing society.” The consequence of all of these changes was the trans-
16:516:42521:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:formation of the Rhineland from an oligarchy-dominated area to one open to new business and
16:517:42612:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:new entrants.  Instead of the traditional oligarchy, in 1810 merchants/manufacturers comprised
16:518:42706:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:about half the members ofConseil General(municipal administrations) in leading Rhineland
16:519:42794:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:towns (Diefendorf, 1980, p.  115), and businesspeople were also well represented in the higher
16:520:42888:18:19:79:241:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:department-levelConseil General.
16:521:42920:19:18:96:525:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Reforms  were  also  systematically  introduced  into  the  German  satellite  kingdoms.   For
16:522:43014:18:19:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:example,  Connelly  (1965,  p.   184)  notes  that  in  the  constitution  of  Westphalia,  which  was
16:523:43116:19:30:79:304:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:issued at Fontainebleau on November 15, 1807,
16:524:43161:30:18:106:498:309:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“  . . .  the  king’s  subjects  were  guaranteed  equality  before  the  law  and  religious
16:525:43254:18:19:106:498:291:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:liberty;  serfdom and feudal rights were abolished;  noble titles were affirmed, but
16:526:43338:19:18:106:498:272:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:were to command no special rights, privileges or offices.  Taxes were to fall equally
16:527:43423:18:19:106:498:254:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on all classes in all parts of the kingdom.  Provision was made for a ministry, council
16:528:43510:19:18:106:498:235:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of state, and a parliament of one hundred (seventy landowners, fifteen merchants
16:529:43590:18:19:106:498:217:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and manufacturers, fifteen savants and distinguished citizens) . . .  Effective January
16:530:43677:19:18:106:498:198:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1,  1808,  theCode  Napol ́eonwas  to  be  the  civil  law.   The  judiciary  was  to  be
16:531:43766:18:18:106:498:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:‘independent’ but was appointed by the king, who could review his appointments
16:532:43844:18:31:106:188:162:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:every five years.”
16:533:43862:31:16:79:525:131:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:He later notes (p.  189) that “all vestiges of feudal law, together with seigneurial and church
16:534:43957:16:15:87:525:115:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:17Prussia’s desire to establish its own control seems to have been one of the reasons why it did not attempt
16:535:44065:15:11:79:525:100:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:to dismantle French reforms in the parts of Western Germany that it took over after 1815 (see Fisher, 1903, pp.
16:536:44176:11:0:79:116:89:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:380-381).
17:537:44187:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:courts,  disappeared  in  the  first  months  of  1808”  and  “All  men  were  equal  before  the  law
17:538:44289:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and eligible for office.” Although the political changes were not implemented (the parliament
17:539:44382:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:hardly  met),  the  economic  changes  were  profound  and—in  this  case  and  in  many  others—
17:540:44479:18:19:79:170:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:largely irreversible.
17:541:44500:19:18:96:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In the Netherlands, Belgium, and Switzerland, Napoleon consolidated the changes brought
17:542:44587:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the Revolution, with an emphasis on establishing (his) legal code and a downplaying of any
17:543:44680:19:18:79:209:560:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:move towards democracy.18
17:544:44705:18:18:96:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Napoleon’s impact on Spain is controversial.  Napoleon acquired nominal control over Spain
17:545:44795:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quite late (1808) and from that time on he had considerable distractions in the form of almost
17:546:44889:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:continual major wars. TheCode Napol ́eonwas supposedly introduced, but it is unclear whether
17:547:44981:18:19:79:525:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:it was ever properly implemented.  There was also considerable popular resistance, abetted by
17:548:45074:19:18:79:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the  British  directly  and  through  their  base  in  Portugal,  and  by  1812  the  French  had  lost
17:549:45177:18:19:79:264:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:effective control south of the Pyrenees.
17:550:45217:19:18:96:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In Italy, the picture is similar with theCode  Napol ́eonbeing implemented mainly in the
17:551:45305:18:29:79:388:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:North, but also involving certain profound changes in the South.
17:552:45369:29:26:79:312:384:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:2.6    Persistence of the French Reforms
17:553:45409:26:18:79:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:After  the  final  collapse  of  Napoleon  in  1815  the  institutional  reforms  implemented  over  the
17:554:45513:18:19:79:525:340:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:previous 25 years suffered various fates.  In our econometric analysis of Germany we specifi-
17:555:45606:19:18:79:525:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cally code reforms throughout the 19th century so examine this issue empirically.  As a first
17:556:45699:18:18:79:525:303:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:approximation, the reforms were most clearly persistent in places where the old elites did not
17:557:45793:18:19:79:525:285:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:return,  for example,  in the areas of Napoleonic Germany that were ceded to Prussia at the
17:558:45884:19:30:79:396:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Congress of Vienna.  For instance, Emsley (2003, pp.  62-63) notes:
17:559:45951:30:19:106:498:236:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“Yet on the left bank of the Rhine, in the departments annexed to France during
17:560:46030:19:18:106:498:217:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the revolutionary decade, the Code became firmly entrenched.  Indeed,  it was so
17:561:46110:18:18:106:498:199:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:well entrenched that, after 1815, the Rhenish elite successfully preserved the Code
17:562:46193:18:19:106:498:181:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and  resisted  attempts  of  their  new  Prussian  masters  to  introduce  the  Prussian
17:563:46281:19:16:106:230:162:11:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:Allgemeines Landrecht.”19
17:564:46306:16:15:87:525:146:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:18Napoleon also briefly invaded Portugal,  but the French presence did not last long,  partly due to British
17:565:46414:15:11:79:525:131:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:intervention.  We therefore treat Portugal as part of the control group, along with Austria (where Napoleon also
17:566:46526:11:7:79:270:120:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:had military victories but never ruled directly).
17:567:46575:7:14:87:525:113:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:19See Sperber (1989) and (1991) for details on the Rhinelanders tried (and succeeded for a long while) to hold
17:568:46685:14:0:79:383:99:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:onto theCode Napol ́eonand other progressive aspects of the French legacy.
18:569:46761:0:18:96:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Emsley’s argument is that the French reforms fundamentally changed the political status
18:570:46848:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quo in such a way as the elites who benefitted from these institutions could sustain the.  The
18:571:46942:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:presence of a new elite created by the reforms and determined to hang onto them is also central
18:572:47037:18:31:79:373:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to the argument of Simms (2004, p.  39) who sums this up as:
18:573:47097:31:18:106:498:585:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:“In  western  and  southern  Germany  there  was  no  going  back  to  the  feudal  sta-
18:574:47185:18:19:106:498:567:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tus  quo  ante.   The  Prussian  bureaucrats  arriving  in  the  newly  acquired  Rhine
18:575:47272:19:18:106:498:548:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Province in 1815 found a population determined to hold onto the French law . . .  In
18:576:47356:18:18:106:498:530:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:southern Germany the old corporate representations had permanently given way
18:577:47432:18:19:106:498:512:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to parliaments whose lower houses were largely elected on the basis of a property
18:578:47513:19:18:106:498:493:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:franchise;  and  by  1820  all  southern  German  states  had  constitutions  guarantee-
18:579:47601:18:19:106:498:475:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ing freedom of conscience and equality before the law . . .  The genie of the reform
18:580:47685:19:18:106:498:456:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:movement—freedom of movement,  the standardization of taxation,  the abolition
18:581:47763:18:30:106:342:438:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:of guilds—could not be put back in the bottle.”20
18:582:47812:30:19:96:525:408:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Although  elsewhere,  most  notably  in  Hanover,  there  was  much  more  of  a  return  to  the
18:583:47909:19:18:79:525:389:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:status quo, these examples highlight a plausible mechanism via which the reforms may have
18:584:47998:18:19:79:525:371:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:had enduring effects, even in places where the de jure aspects of French reforms were repealed
18:585:48092:19:34:79:130:352:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:after 1815.
18:586:48103:34:29:79:138:318:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:3    Data
18:587:48112:29:19:79:525:289:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In this section, we briefly describe the four types of data we use for our empirical analysis.  In
18:588:48210:19:29:79:372:270:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:all cases, further details are contained in the Data Appendix.
18:589:48272:29:25:79:375:241:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:3.1    Country and Polity Level Outcome Variables
18:590:48321:25:19:79:524:216:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:We use two main data series to measure economic prosperity across countries and polities.21
18:591:48412:19:16:79:525:197:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our first measure of prosperity is the urbanization rate of a country or a polity, measured as
18:592:48506:16:15:87:525:181:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:20Note that the southern states mentioned here—Baden, W ̈urttemberg, and Bavaria—are not in our treatment
18:593:48611:15:11:79:525:166:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:group of invaded countries.  To the extent that defensive modernization promoted economic growth, this will
18:594:48718:11:7:79:267:155:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:bias our result downwards, as we discuss later.
18:595:48765:7:14:87:525:148:6:g_d0_f4:9:g_d0_f5:21Our units of observation are countries in their present-day borders where applicable,  i.e.,  where political
18:596:48876:14:11:79:525:134:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:entities  during  the  19th  century  are  roughly  corresponding  to  today’s  borders.   For  the  cases  of  Italy  and
18:597:48998:11:11:79:525:123:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:Germany,  two  complicating  factors  occur:   on  the  one  hand,  different  regions  of  these  countries  experience
18:598:49118:11:11:79:525:112:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:different types of French ‘treatment.’  On the other hand, Italy and Germany are established as unitary states
18:599:49228:11:11:79:525:101:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:only towards the end of the 19th century.  Therefore, we consider 8 distinct pre-unitary polities in Germany,
18:600:49337:11:0:79:381:90:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:and 12 such polities in Italy.  For more details, refer to appendices 6 and 6.
19:601:49417:0:18:79:525:671:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the percent of the population living in cities with more than 5,000 inhabitants.  We compute
19:602:49509:18:19:79:525:653:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:urbanization rates for 41 polities (21 present-day states, 8 German and 12 Italian pre-unitary
19:603:49603:19:18:79:525:634:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:states).  Bairoch (1988, Chapter 1) and de Vries (1984, p.  164) argue that only areas with high
19:604:49699:18:19:79:525:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:agricultural productivity and a developed transportation network could support large urban
19:605:49789:19:18:79:525:597:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:populations.  In addition, Acemoglu, Johnson and Robinson (2002) present evidence that both
19:606:49880:18:19:79:525:579:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in the time-series and the cross-section there is a close association between urbanization and
19:607:49974:19:18:79:525:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:income per capita before as well as after industrialization.  We take urbanization as the best
19:608:50068:18:18:79:525:542:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:available proxy for GDP per capita.  Urbanization estimates from Bairoch (1988) and the city
19:609:50160:18:19:79:525:524:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:level population data from Bairoch, Batou and Ch`evre (1988) are fairly reliable and build on
19:610:50253:19:18:79:525:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the work of many economic historians.22In addition, urbanization at this stage of European
19:611:50343:18:19:79:518:487:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:development is likely highly correlated with the growth of industrial activity based in cities.
19:612:50438:19:18:96:525:468:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We also use estimates of GDP per capita for national polities from Maddison (2003).  The
19:613:50526:18:19:79:525:450:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:numbers before 1820 are in many cases educated guesses by Maddison.  Furthermore, because
19:614:50615:19:18:79:525:431:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:these  estimates  are  constructed  for  modern  polities,  such  as  Germany  or  Italy,  they  do  not
19:615:50719:18:18:79:525:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:allow us to exploit the considerable variation within Germany and Italy.  Maddison’s estimates
19:616:50813:18:19:79:525:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:start in 1500, and are available for 1600, 1700, 1820, and then more frequently.  We use the
19:617:50905:19:18:79:492:376:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:results with the GDP per capita estimates as a check on our urbanization rate results.
19:618:50991:18:19:96:525:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:For Germany, we also collected data from the 19th century of the extent of railway networks
19:619:51082:19:18:79:525:339:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and also the distribution of the population across different sectors of the economy (industry,
19:620:51176:18:29:79:499:321:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:commerce and agriculture).  The sources for all these variables are listed in Appendix 6.
19:621:51265:29:26:79:262:292:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:3.2    Coding French Treatment
